PREFACE.
The numerous corrections and additions, which the catalogue 
of British plants has of late received, in consequence, of the 
prevailing taste for botanical pursuits, having rendered the 
Enchiridion Botanicum of Broughton, and all similar publica- 
tions, very insufficient for the purposes of the practical botan- 
ist, the Author was induced, in the beginning of last year, to 
prepare the volume, now offered to the publick, for the press.
Had he then been apprized, that a Pocket Flora would so 
soon appear by another hand, he would have relinquished his 
undertaking; but having nearly completed the work, before 
the Synopsis of Mr. Symons was announced for publication ; 
and finding that work written in the Latin language, less com- 
prehensive in its plan, and not continued through the three 
last Orders of the Class Cryptogamia, he could not consider it, 
as superseding the necessity of this volume ; which, he flat- 
ters himself, will prove an useful companion in a botanical ex- 
cursion.
In the Catalogue here given will be found all the Species con- 
tained in the third edition of Dr. Withering’s very valuable 
work on the botany of these kingdoms, together with such ad- 
11 
ditional ones, as have been since discovered and ascertained..
1 
All the Varieties also are given, except those depending merely 
upon size, or colour.
In the Arrangement the Author has adhered strictly to the'* 
method established by the justly celebrated Linnaaus, in prefer-- 
ence to the reformed system of Professor Thunberg ; although i 
this has been adopted on the continent, by Haenke in his edi-.
tion of the Genera Plantarum, and by Willdenow in his Florae 
Berolinensis Prodromus ; and in this kingdom by Sibthorp, 
"Withering, and Symons.
That he may not unnecessarily add to 
the size of this volume, he will assign his reasons for this, and give 
Some observations on the botanical language, here employed, in 
the Preface to a work, containing an Introduction to Botany, 
and the Natural Characters of the Genera of British Plants, 
which is now in the press : and will content himself with giv- 
ing here the following short statement of the plan of the British 
Flora.
| 1 .
. \ • I ' r * ; " -“I 
The Genera are numbered in the same manner, as in Reich- 
ard’s edition of the Genera Plantarum, and the fourteenth edi- 
tion of the Systema Vegetabilium, published by Murray, at 
Gottingen, in 1784.
The Characters are in general translated from the works of 
Linnaeus, and chiefly from the Systema Vegetabilium.
Where 
Linnean characters are wanting, the deficiency has been suppli- 
ed from the works of Hudson, Lightfoot, Withering, Dickson, 
Smith, the Linnean Transactions, &c.
&c.
'With these are oc- 
casionally given some additional distinctive marks, either in- 
cluded in a parenthesis, or subjoined as an observation.
To each Species is added, 
1. The English Name-, except in the three last orders of the 
Class Cryptogamia, where they have been almost universally 
omitted, because they are mere translations, and not properly 
established.
2. The General Habitation^ or Situation ; in which it is found, 
Ill 
and in some instances where the plant vs very rare, the particu- 
lar place is indicated.
t 
3. The Duration ; which is expressed by the initials of the 
words Annual, Biennial, Perennial, Shrub, Tree.
4 .
The Season, or Months of Flowering; the months being ex- 
pressed by numbers, e.g. January by 1, February by 2, &c.
&c.
5. A Reference to some Figure, or Figures.
In general one fi- 
gure only is referred to, and in some instances an inferior Eng- 
lish figure has been preferred to a superior foreign one, as being 
more accessible to the generality of readers.
When two or 
more are given, the first place has not always been assigned to 
the best.
The Synonyms of Hudson, Lightfoot, and Withering, are also 
constantly added, when they differ from the Linnean name, or 
from each other.
And the Synonyms of other authors are fre- 
quently given, especially in the Class Cryptogamia.
To such species as are doubtful natives , a note of interrogation 
is affixed.

Obs. The figure of the leaves varies, according as the plant is 
more or less completely submersed, &c.
thus Var. 1.
passes 
into Var. a.
when growing in moist places, which have been 
overflowed in the winter; and in the botanic garden at Ox- 
ford has been observed to change into Var. 3.
; so that how- 
ever different the plants, belonging to this genus, may appear 
in the form of their leaves, mode of fructification, or time of 
flowering, there is not any good ground for considering them, 
as specifically distinct.— Hudson has transferiedCallitriche to 
the Class Polygamia.

1- 
celled, 5-valved at the apex.

merous.
Oil. This genus has been removed by Hudson to the Order 
Hexagyma, and, as far as relates to the British species, with 
propriety; for in no one instance have I found 5 pist.: 
the prevailing number is 6, and wc not unfrequently find 
8. It has not occurred to me to observe 8 pist.
more fre- 
quently in D.
anghca, than in longijolia, but rather the 
contrary ; and 1 have never met with 8 in D.
rotundifj- 
lia.
When the number of pist.
is 6, they always go off in 
g pairs, each pair being united at the base ; when 8, in 4 
pairs; and the number of valves corresponds with the 
the number of pairs of pist., being 3 when the pist.
are 
6, 4 when they are 8, never 5, as is stated by 1 in.
in the 
character given above.
What I have here asserted is to 
be understood only of the British plants.

Obs. a.
L’Heritier has divided this genus into three, viz.
Geranium, Pelargonium, and Erodium.
To the first he re- 
fers all the species which have 10 perfect stam., to the second 
all those which have 7, and to the third all those which have 
5 only.
As we have no British species with 7 stam,, it is 
unnecessary to give the char, of Pelargonium here ; but the 
character of Erodium, as comprehending the division Myrr- 
hina, is annexed, 
---Erodium.
Cal. 5-leaved.
Cor. 5-petalled.
Nect.
scales 5, .
alternating with the fiiam, and nectariferous Glands sit- 
ting on the base of the stamens.
Fruit 5-grained, ros- 
trate, beaks spiral, bearded inwards.
L’Heritiek.
1 
And also the new character of Geranium, 
Geranium.
Cal. 5-leaved.
Cor. pctalled, regular.
Ncct.
glands 5, melliferous, adhering to the base of the longer 
fiiam.
Fruit 5-grained, rostrate, beaks simple, naked, 
(neither spiral, nor) bearded.
L’Heritier.

MONOGAMIA.
1C0 5 * J ASI ° nf..
Common Calyx 10-leaved, Cor.
5-petalled, regular.
Caps, inferior, a-cell- 
ed (many-seeded, crowned by the proper 
calyx).
1. Jasione.
Oh. The economy of the (lowers in this genus is very singu- 
lar.
The florets of the disk have fertile anthers, which are 
united with one another only at the base, and club-shaped 
barren hirsute pistils ; on the contrary the florets of the ray, 
which arc furnished with true emarginate stigmas, proper 
for fertilizing the seeds, have barren stamens ; hence the 
proper stigmas receive the pollen from the club-shaped ones, 
upon which it is first recc ived, as they pass through the an- 
thers.

Obs. The heads of (lowers arc considerably smaller than in 
S. simplex, and not so numerous, being generally 2 or 3, 
very seldom more ; when three, the two uppermost are 
sessile, the lowest pedunclcd.
In some instances the 
leaves are shorter than the stein, and in that case are found 
completely submersed after heavy falls of rain, with the 
spikes just above the water.
When growing entirely 
out of the water, the plants arc very dwarfish.
I have one 
specimen in fructification not more than four inches ia 
height.

3-sided, within the nectary.
Obs. The part which is called the nectary in the generic char., 
Lin. has named the capsule in the specific characters; but 
not very properly, as it is perfectly independent of the fruit.
Ur. Smith considers it as a permanent corol.
To me the 
terms nectary and corol appear less exceptionable than cap- 
sule !
however I have chosen to adopt the last, as it is em- 
ployed by Dr. Goodenough, in his two excellent papers on 
the British Car.ces inserted in the second and third vol.
of 
the Lin. Trans., from which the arrangement and charac- 
ters, &c.
of the speciesherc given are borrowed.
Obs. 2.
Lin. has distributed the species of Carex in the fol- 
lowing manner, substituting the term spike for ament.
1. Spike single, simple.
2. Spikes androgynous.
3. Spikes distinct as to their sex, the females sessile.
4. Spikes distinct as to their sex, the fem peduncled.
5. Spikes distinct as to their sex , male spikes more than one.
1. Spike single, simple.
C. Spike simple, dioicous, margins of the capsule 
serrulated.

Obs. All the species with androgynous spikes have their 
stigma bifid, except C.
pauciflora ; and those with distinct 
male and female .spikes have it trifid, except C.
pulla, 
cccspitosa, rigida, and stricta.
Obs. 2.
The investigation of the different species of Carex 
is rendered difficult by an occasional variation in the dis- 
tinctive marks, c.
g. the length of the foltaceous bractes 
is not absolutely constant; male flowers are sometimes 
found in the female spikes, and vice versa ; the peduncle 
of the female spikes, which in some species is generally 
sessile, is at other times found of considerable length, and 
the division of the caps, at the summit is sometimes pre- 
sent, sometimes wanting.
Obs. 3.
All the Carices are believed by Dr. Goodenough 
to dispose of their seeds by the opening of the point of 
their capsule, but in some this opening is not observable

SCHREBER Gen. PI.
Obs. Hedwig has very satisfactorily shewn that this genus 
by no means ought to retain a place amongst the A {usd.
where it was ranked by Linnaeus.
And since the fruc- 
tifications resemble those of Osmunda, and arc mostly 
disposed in spikes, I have chosen rather to place it 
amongst the Spiked Fi/ices, than to adopt the heteroge- 
neous Order of Miscellanea: of Schreber.
Some of the 
species indeed are not properly spiked, and amongst 
these is one British species, viz.
L. Selago ; but upon the 
■whole this appears to me the most convenient place for 
the genus.
That I might not however disappoint those 
who prefer considering it as belonging to the Order 
Musci, 1 have also given it its usual place in the system, 
with the Linnean char., and a reference to the Filices.
— Prof.
Schreber has introduced into his new Order 
of Miscellanea:, Lycopodium, Equisetum, lhlularia, 
and Isoetes, together with three other genera, not hilh* 
erto discovered in Britain.

05s.
Lin. is not so exact as might be wished in the use of 
terms in his descriptions of the different species of Poly- 
podium, Asplcmum, Sec. one instance has already oc- 
curred, what are denominated lobes in the subdivision 
being named pinna: in the specific char.
Notwithstand- 
ing this want of precision, the Linmcan char, are here 
translated literally, but in order to facilitate the investi- 
gation of these difficult genera, in the additional char., 
and distinctive marks, a more precise language is employ- 
ed, of which it will be necessary to give a short explana- 
tion.
A Frond , in this division at least, consists of a stipe 
and a leaf or leaves, but the term Frond is commonly 
used synonimously with Leaf.
A Simple Frond has a stipe and a leaf, e.
g. Asplenium 
Scolopcndrium.
A Pinnatijid Frond has a stipe, leaf, and lobes, e.
g. P.
vu/gare.
A Pinnate Frond has a stipe, leaf, and leaflets, e.
g. P.
Lonchitis.
An Almost Bipinnate Frond has a stipe, leaf, pinnatifid 
leaflets, and lobes, e.
g. P.
F. mas.
A Bipinnate Frond has a stipe, leaf, pinnae,.
and leaflets, 
the leaflets either simple, as m P.
The/ypteris, or 
pinnatifid, with deeply cut lobes, as in P.
F .Fxmina.
A Super-decompound Frond has a stipe, pinnae, pinnule, 
and leaflets, the leaflets simple, or lobed ; or if we 
consider the stipe as supporting more than one leaf, 
then the leaves are bipinnate, as in P.
Dryoptcris.

Obs. 1.
The present character of Mniyni not being sufficient- 
ly determinate to admit of any species being referred to it 
with a proper degree of certainty, and the frequent removal 
of the species of Mnium to Bryum, and v.
v., having oc- 
casioned much confusion and perplexity, I have thought it 
better to transfer for the present all the species ranked by 
CRYPTOGAM I A.
MUSCI.
Mnium.
251 
Linnxus as Mniums, to the other genera.
Weber has 
gone so far as to entirely omit the genus Mnium, but 
I have judged it proper for the convenience of those who 
are in the habit of considering certain species as Mniums, 
to retain the whole of the species enumerated by Lin.
together with their characters, and to give a reference to 
Bryura, Polytrichum, &c.
for a further description of 
them.

can be derived in the investigation ot the species from 
referring them to more than one genus, the arrangement 
of Goodenough and Woodward (which is nearly the 
same as Hudson’s), and also the characicis, &c.
of the 
species as given in a valuable paper inserted in the 3d

of the Lin. Trans., are here adopted.

Obs. F.
<ricoid.cs, granulatus, and sclaginoides are so much alike 
that very great attention is necessary to’ distinguish them.— 
Encoidcs is of low stature, erect like a little shrub, and ven 
bushy, and the tubercles are clustered at the ends of the 
bianchlcts like grapes.
— Granulatus and sclaginoides are tall., 
er and more infirm.
Sclaginoides has its tubercles solitary 
and rather distant, at the base of the leaves of the extreme: 
branches, but granulatus has them contiguous, and set close-- 
ly all along the extreme branches, and part of the large: 
branches.
The leaves in all are subulate and acute, but ir 
sclaginoides they arc four times larger than the tubercles, ir 
ericoides twice as large, and in granulatus not much larger

In the Water Gate, Burks.
9. Bolt, dr.
Descr.
The Root a little swelled, and sending off brown fibres.
Stalk cylindrical, often curved, sometimes quite erect, of a 
pale tawny brown on the outside, while young solid, but 
soon becoming hollow, the substance of the flesh white and 
spungy.
Curtain cobweb-like, vanishing in the infancy of 
-the plant, and leaving no vestige.
Gills 3, or 4 in a set, 
tawny-brown, numerous, brittle, straight, and adhering by 
their base to the top of the stalk.
Pileus of the same colourr 
as the gills, darkest near the centre.
In dry weather then 
outer coat in full grown plants is cracked, and torn in vari- 
ous directions, exposing the internal white substance to 
view.

Bull.
352.
Bolt.
73.
Obs. This plant in the first stages of its growth is of a cir- 
cular figure, growing horizontally with the gill* upwards, 
the gills at this time are distinct and branched, and theii 
sides arc united by small lateral processes.
As it ad- 
vances in growth the pileus separates from the wood, till 
it only adheres bv one edge, and gradually takes a hori- 
zontal position with the gills downwards.
After some 
time the small lateral processes encrease so much as to 
equal the gills in height and substance, and the gills be- 
come transformed into oblong pores.
In this state it 
connects the Agarici with the Boleti.
See Bolton, who 
has very accurately described this change in the position 
and structure of the plant, and illustiated it with figures.
Var. 2.
Pileus green, soft, clothy.
Bolt.
73.
d. 
Is the green coat a species of Byssus ?
Meru'lius.
(Haller.) Pileus with 
veins underneath.
0bs.
This genus is allotted a more comprehensive charac- 
ter by Dr. Withering than by Haller, for besides the 
species, which are veined underneath, he refers it t» 
384 CRYPTOGAMIA.
FUNGI.
Merulius.
those, which have gills of the same substance with the rest 
of the plant.
It would be extremely desirable to diminish 
the number of species of Agaricus by the establishment oi 
another genus, provided a character could be constructed 
sufficiently definite to admit of the species being referred to 
it with certainty, but unless this be effected, the investiga- 
tion will be rendered more difficult instead of being facili- 
tated.
And I am of opinion that little or no advantage has 
been gained by the attempt of Dr. Withering, because in 
the first place, it is by no means easy to determine which 
plants have, gills of the same substance with the rest of the 
plant; and secondly, the number of species removed hv 
him from the genus Agaribus to Merulius does not exceed 
nine, all of which may be made out without much difficulty 
as Agarics by means of his very judicious subdivisions of 
that genus.
Besides-there appears to me much more reason 
for dividing the genus Merulius of Withering into two, than 
the genus Agaricus of Linnaius, for the Agarics with gills 
.of the same substance, arc much more closely allied to those 
with gills of a different substance, than the veined Merulii 
to the gilled ones.
However for the convenience of those 
who are accustomed to Dr. Witherings distribution of this 
tribe of plants, I have subjoined the character, of Merulius 
as given by him, and a syn.opsis of the species of his first 
division, with references to the pages where thev arc de- 
scribed.

Fungus campanulate, sessile.
( Fungus slightly concave, hemispheri- 
cal, or campanulate ; sessile, or stipi- 
tate.) 
Cbs. The illustrious author of the sexual system has been 
more attenttve to external form, and general appearance, 
than to the nature of the fructification, in constructing 
the generic char, of the Fungi, although the latter is un- 
doubtedly the true ground of generic distinction.
A 
striking instance of this occurs in the genus now under 
consideration, for we find associated here plants possess- 
ing three different kinds of fructification.
In the first 
place we have P.
Untijcra, which is a campanulate fun- 
gus, closed at the mouth, and containing capsules attach- 
ed by pedicels to the bottom of the bell.
.Secondly, we 
find P.
punctata , in which the seeds are collected in tu- 
bercles, imbedded in the upper surface of the plant.
— 
Thirdly wc have P.
scutcllata, in which the seeds are 
situated on the upper surface, but not collected in any 
kind of pericarp.
1. Sessile, campanulate, closed when young, and 
containing pedicelled lentil-shaped capsules.
1. P.
Campanulate, lentil-bearing (pale mouse-col., 
smooth within, darker, roughish without, h.
i to 1).
Nidularia campanulata.

Obs. 1.
When it is in its early stale, the upper half of the bell 
is occupied by a vesicle containing a fluid, which appeal 
to be discharged downwards, after which the uppeT part 
cracks, and the capsules are discovered connected by 
filiform pedicels to the bottom of the bell.
— Sometimes 
the membrane which clpses the mouth of the bell is de- 
tached all round, and thrown off.
See Bolt, and Sower.
Obs. 2.
The'plants comprized in this division are referred 
by Bulliard to a genus which he has named Nidularia, 
of which, and Peziza, he has furnished the annexed cha- 
racters.
Nidularia.
Fungus small, membranous, coriaceous, 
sessile, cup-like, or inversely campanulate, con- 
taining very broad, lentil-shaped, pedicelled seeds 
in the bottom.
Peziza.

P.385..
oi'des.
Aur icuia'r ia.
Fungus flattish, mem- 
branous; when young, smooth above, 
villous underneath ; when advanced, 
resupinate, or concave.
Seeds on the 
smooth surface.
Obs. 1.
The plants belonging to this genus remain attached 
by some one part to the substance on which they origin- 
ally grew.
When they adhere by one side, the situation 
of their surfaces is cdmpletely reversed ; when they ad- 
here by the centre, the margin is gradually elevated, they 
become concave like PezU*, and Lhe vilious surface be- 
comes the outer one.
Obs. 2.
This genus is adopted rather with a view of facilita- 
ting the investigation, thau from a conviction of the ne- 
cessity of it.
For it has been shewn above that the stalk- 
less Agarici and Bolcti undergo the same change in the 
situation of their surfaces, and there is nothing peculiar 
in the fructification.
We might thercfoic refer the dif- 
ferent species to Boletus, Merulius, or Helvetia, accord- 
ing as the seed-bearing surface is found to be porous, 
veined, or even.

Obs. 1, Every one, especially in var.
2., would at first 
sight assert with Bulliard and others, that the powder 
is intermixed with filaments , and enclosed in a capsule ; 
but it will appear upon a more accurate investigation, 
that the fibrils are formed from the seeds being fallen 
into a powder, and that the glume, by being so much 
distended, has assumed a silvery whiteness, and the 
form of a covering.
